SUBJECT:
Metrics for Local Transportation Analysis for New Development – Use of Level of Service and Vehicle Miles Traveled
RECOMMENDATION:
Receive an informational report on metrics for local transportation analysis, provide direction to staff on future 
amendments to the current General Plan, and consider approval of staff’s recommendations regarding vehicle miles 
traveled analysis.
BACKGROUND:
Transportation analysis conducted by the City under the California Environmental Quality Act (CEQA) has historically 
evaluated Level of Service (LOS) and vehicle delay at intersections to assess localized traffic impacts as a result of planned 
development. Changes to CEQA as a result of the passage of California Senate Bill 743 (SB 743) require cities by July 1, 
2020 to use Vehicle Miles Traveled (VMT) as the metric to assess transportation impacts in CEQA analysis of development. 
Cities are no longer allowed to use LOS as a metric under CEQA to measure impacts associated with transportation.  In 
order to comply with the statute and on an interim basis until the City has its own adopted VMT policy, the City is currently 
utilizing the guidelines published by the State of California Office of Policy and Research (OPR) utilizing VMT to assess 
transportation impacts under CEQA.   The City is currently developing a transportation analysis policy that will replace OPR 
guidelines to better align City needs with SB 743.  In the interim period prior to the City’s adoption of the City’s own VMT 
policy, staff will analyze new development using the OPR Guidelines as recommended below.
By eliminating LOS as a metric under CEQA, the City can no longer rely solely on CEQA to require mitigation measures to 
offset all transportation-related effects of development.  Instead, separately from the VMT analysis required under CEQA, 
the City staff intends to use development impact fees and exactions based upon its police power to require improvements 
to offset LOS-related effects.  SB 743 does not prescribe the method by which cities conduct transportation analysis for 
local impacts or for conformance with locally-adopted plans. 
The City’s current Circulation Element of the General Plan contains numerous policies requiring that development projects 
adhere to a stated LOS standard.  Specifically, Policy C2.1 identifies the City’s standard as “Maintain a Level of Service no 
worse than mid-LOS D, average delay of 45.0 seconds, as the acceptable Level of Service for all intersections within the 
City.” Further, Policy C2.7 details requirements for development projects to fund off-site circulation improvements if the 
project-generated traffic degrades the performance of the transportation network below these thresholds. 
Currently, in order to determine when transportation improvements beyond payment of the City’s transportation impact 
fee (TIF) will be required, the Circulation Element states that additional improvements will be required when traffic 
impacts have been deemed ”significant” in a CEQA analysis.  General Plan Policy C2.7 defines “significance” by LOS.  The 
268 of 328

--- page 269 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
CEQA-specific language in the General Plan policies and the requirement for use of VMT under CEQA limit the City’s ability 
to utilize these policies to implement transportation improvements to negate adverse effects of increased congestion.
The City, led by the Community Development Department, is currently updating the General Plan, which is expected to be 
completed in 2023. Through this update process, the City will have the opportunity to evaluate potential new 
transportation metrics, to assess local effects of development projects outside of CEQA, and to consider if LOS is the 
transportation metric for use in the future. However, during this interim time period between when the new VMT policy is 
adopted and when General Plan 2040 will be complete, staff recommends modifications to allow the City to continue with 
local transportation analysis (e.g., LOS analysis) in addition to the analysis conducted under CEQA (e.g., VMT analysis).  To 
this end, staff recommends the following approach toward bridging current policies with the long-term policies that will be 
tied to the new General Plan.

Short-Term: Slight modifications to the local transportation analysis process to require development projects to 
fund circulation improvements needed to offset any adverse LOS effects. To effectuate this change, an 
amendment to the General Plan’s Circulation Element eliminating links between LOS and CEQA findings would be 
required.  As discussed further below, staff requests input regarding Council’s preferred transportation analyses to 
support such findings.

Mid-Term: The City will evaluate its Transportation Impact Fee (TIF) to identify potentially appropriate updates to 
project nexus, traffic analysis process, transportation infrastructure improvement projects, and total TIF fee 
amount.  At this time, additional projects focused on bicycle and pedestrian access would be included. 
Additionally, staff will evaluate alternative TIF structures that might better suit the needs and goals of the City 
(e.g., different TIF fee and structure in transit-oriented development areas).

Long-Term: As part of the development of the Circulation Element for General Plan 2040, transportation metrics 
for local transportation analysis will be evaluated to potentially identify alternative metrics to LOS that may better 
meet the City’s needs and goals. 
Staff has evaluated current efforts by other local jurisdictions regarding local transportation analysis and identified short-
term options for the City Council to consider.
Current Efforts by Other Local Jurisdictions

Redwood City:  All projects will be reviewed for consistency with locally-adopted plans. The review will include off-
site traffic operations assessed utilizing LOS in a smaller geographic area than current practice. In the event the 
LOS analysis identifies an adverse traffic operations effect associated with the project, improvements would be 
required to be implemented to offset the effects. If the improvement is related to increasing roadway capacity, a 
supplemental VMT analysis will be conducted to ensure the project does not cause a CEQA impact. 

Mountain View:  Projects will be reviewed through a local transportation analysis process utilizing LOS to address 
traffic operations effects and will be required to implement operational improvements to offset any negative 
effects. There are provisions for small projects to be screened from being required to conduct the local 
transportation analysis, in line with those that are not required to conduct VMT analysis under CEQA due to an 
assumption of less than significant impact.

San Jose:  Projects are required to conduct LOS analysis for the Local Transportation Analysis requirements. The 
LOS analysis focuses on a smaller geographic area specifically focused on evaluating project access and circulation.

Palo Alto:  Projects will be required to conduct LOS analysis for conformance with local policies. The LOS analysis is 
in a more limited capacity than current practice and is based on project access and circulation. 
Short-Term Options for Local Transportation Analysis
1.
Maintain Existing Practices:
o
Methodology: Conduct LOS analysis for all intersections and roadways that may see adverse effects from 
the proposed development project.
o
Considerations: Projects may require analysis of far-reaching locations to assess full potential effects, 
269 of 328

--- page 270 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
which is a costly and time-intensive process. 
2.
Maintain LOS in Reduced Capacity:
o
Methodology: Conduct LOS analysis for a smaller geographic area, based on distance from project 
location, trips generated, or other means. 
o
Considerations: A limited LOS analysis is focused on project access and ensures that the analysis required 
correlates to the size of the project proposed. 
3.
Exempt Projects within TOD Areas from LOS Transportation Analysis:
o
Methodology: Conduct LOS analysis for all projects outside of the TOD areas
o
Considerations: Reduces burden of traffic analysis on infill development projects but may negate or not 
fully represent the traffic effects of the project on the roadway network. 
4.
Remove LOS as Transportation Metric:
o
Methodology: Remove LOS entirely as a local transportation metric, and conduct VMT analysis only.
o
Considerations: Assessing consistency with General Plan 2030 would not be feasible without significant 
General Plan modifications, and assessing fees under the TIF would not be feasible because the TIF is 
based on a nexus to LOS standards in the General Plan.
Staff seeks direction from City Council regarding options for use of LOS for local transportation analysis in the short-term  
period to assess consistency of development projects with the City’s General Plan 2030, while the mid- and long-term 
options for transportation metrics are determined as part of updating the TIF and the General Plan 2040. Staff believes 
Option 2, which allows the City to maintain LOS in some capacity for future local transportation analysis, provides an 
appropriate interim methodology.  If supported by Council, staff would bring proposed General Plan amendments and 
local transportation analysis guidelines, including the methodology for determining the smaller geographic area, in 
conjunction with the new VMT policy. 
OPR Guidelines
Staff is currently developing the framework for the City’s transportation analysis policy to align with SB 743. As part of this 
effort, a decision is necessary to determine when a transportation analysis under CEQA is required. OPR released a 
Technical Advisory during December 2018 to provide general direction to lead agencies on implementation 
methodologies. OPR has included recommendations for screening criteria to determine when projects can be assumed to 
have a less than significant impact under CEQA.  These screening criteria include:

Small projects,

Projects located near high-quality transit, and

Projects consisting of 100% affordable housing in infill locations.
Small projects are defined by OPR as those that generate fewer than 110 trips per day.  The threshold for project size may 
be modified or the criterion may be changed by the jurisdiction.  Some jurisdictions utilize number of units or square 
footage of development as the criterion. 
The screening criteria provided by OPR includes consideration of project location and provides guidance that affects 
projects near high-quality transit, i.e., within ½ mile of a major transit stop (e.g. the City’s three Caltrain stations) or within 
½ mile of a stop on a high quality transit corridor (e.g. SamTrans ECR stops on El Camino Real).  Projects near high-quality 
transit can, per OPR’s recommendation, generally be presumed to have less than significant VMT impact and may be 
“screened out” from transportation analysis as part of the CEQA process. In doing so, projects in these areas benefit from a 
streamlined CEQA process, although local plan conformance may still be considered (e.g., LOS impacts).  However, the 
presumption that a project near high-quality transit does not result in a VMT impact does not apply if project- or location-
specific information indicates the project will still generate significant levels of VMT.  
OPR cites data indicating the provision of affordable housing generally improves a jobs-housing balance, which results in 
reduced VMT through shortened commutes. Further, OPR states that a project consisting of a high-percentage of 
affordable housing may be a basis for the lead agency to find a less than significant impact on VMT.  OPR’s recommended 
screening criterion supports a presumption of less than significant impact for a 100 percent affordable residential 
270 of 328

--- page 271 ---
CITY OF SAN MATEO
development in infill locations. 
Some jurisdictions are requiring that all projects conduct VMT analyses as part of CEQA and do not allow screening.  Staff 
requests City Council input regarding the use of this screening criteria in the upcoming City policy, and feedback regarding 
any additions or modifications to the VMT screening criteria that should be considered as part of this policy development. 
In addition to the above, the OPR guidelines provide further technical recommendations for assessment of VMT. 

Thresholds of significance: OPR recommends specific thresholds based on certain land use categories to 
determine when a project has a significant impact related to transportation.  Staff recommends using the 
following OPR-recommended thresholds of significance.
o
Residential: 15% below regional average per capita VMT
o
Office: 15% below regional average per employee VMT
o
Retail: No net change in VMT in the study area

Mixed Use Projects: OPR recommends analyzing each land use separately, or the project’s dominant land use 
only.  Staff recommends applying whichever method will result in the most accurate assessment of a project’s 
traffic impacts.

Trip-Based v. Tour-Based: OPR guidelines allow the option for type of assessment utilized. Trip-based assessment 
is based on individual trips to and from the project site. Tour-based assessment includes the full trip from home 
(and back), including the new project. The guideline from OPR is tour-based assessment is ideal if available, but 
the type of assessment must be constant for the full analysis and in comparing to thresholds.  This will be 
determined by staff on a project-by-project basis dependent on available data. 
Staff requests City Council concurrence on the use of the OPR guidelines and thresholds included therein for VMT analysis 
until such time as the City-specific policy is brought for adoption, expected in July 2020. 
BUDGET IMPACT:
There is no fiscal impact for this informational item. 
ENVIRONMENTAL DETERMINATION:
In accordance with Public Resources Code Section 21065, providing direction regarding proposed future General Plan 
amendments is  not  a project subject to CEQA, because the City Council is providing direction to staff and not taking action 
at this time.  Also, in accordance with Public Resources Code Section 21065, approving staff’s recommendations regarding 
interim implementation of the OPR Guidelines in order to conform with SB 743 is not a project subject to CEQA, because 
staff’s recommendations relate to the methods that will be used to evaluate the transportation impacts of proposed 
developments and will not independently create impacts.  In addition, in accordance with CEQA Guidelines Section 
15378(b)(5), the City Council’s approval of staff’s recommendations with regard to analyzing transportation impacts is an 
administrative activity that will not result in direct or indirect physical changes to the environment and therefore not a 
project subject to CEQA.

All meeting noticing requirements were met.

SUBJECT:
Ballot Measure Council Ad Hoc Subcommittee – Recommendation 
RECOMMENDATION:
Oppose the initiative measure extending Measure P height limits for 10 years. 
BACKGROUND:
The City Council will take action at this meeting to call an election on an initiative measure that qualified for the ballot and 
was certified by the Council at its July 16, 2018 meeting. 
At its May 4, 2020 meeting, the City Council convened an ad hoc sub-committee of the Mayor and Deputy Mayor to make 
a recommendation on whether the City Council should support or oppose the ballot measure. 
The sub-committee met and is recommending that the Council oppose the initiative measure extending Measure P height 
limits for 10 years for the following reason: The subcommittee cites that the City is engaged in its General Plan process, an 
extensive and robust community outreach process to give all residents a voice in the future of San Mateo. The City Council 
is committed to letting all residents' voices be heard during this process and community input will dictate future zoning, 
including heights and densities and proximity to transit. Since the City Council will not change zoning until the General Plan 
is finished, passing measure P at this time would stifle the process and discourage participation. For these reasons, we 
encourage the City Council to oppose the measure and for residents to vote NO on this measure. 
BUDGET IMPACT:
There is no budget impact with this action. 
ENVIRONMENTAL DETERMINATION:
This administrative action is not a project subject to CEQA, because it is an organizational or administrative activity that 
will not result in direct or indirect physical changes in the environment. (CEQA Guidelines Section 15378(b)(5).)

SUBJECT:

RECOMMENDATION:
Adopt a Resolution to call for and conduct the November 3, 2020 General Municipal Election; request consolidation with 
San Mateo County for election services and authorize the City Clerk to execute the Service Agreement for the Provision of 
Election Services with the County of San Mateo; and adopt a Resolution to place the measure to Extend Measure P on the 
ballot.   
BACKGROUND:
A general municipal election for the City of San Mateo is scheduled for November 3, 2020 per the City’s charter. This is the 
first even-year general election since the City amended the charter in 2017 as a result of the requirements of SB415 to 
move to even-year elections to take advantage of higher voter turnout in Presidential or Gubernatorial election years. A 
resolution is provided with this report that would call the election, request consolidation with the County for the conduct 
of the election, and identify the purpose of the election to elect two City Council Members for the following offices, 
currently held by:

Diane Papan

Amourence Lee
The City is also placing a measure on the ballot as a result of the Certificate of Sufficiency approved at its July 16, 2018 
Regular City Council Meeting for the initiative titled “Extension of Measure P” (Attachment 2 and Attachment 3). The full 
text of the proposed measure is appended to the Resolution as Exhibit A in both alternative resolutions. The difference 
between the two attachments is Attachment 3 (Alternate B) requests full text publication of the measure and Attachment 
2 (Alternate A) requests the County publish a link to the City’s website where a voter can source to the full text of the 
measure. Council is asked to choose which resolution to pass.
BUDGET IMPACT:
Attachment 3 is an estimate for election costs from the County of San Mateo Election Services. It is based on cost per 
registered voter (prv) and is provided in a range of $2.71 prv to $3.25 prv. Our current voter registration is 56,192. That 
puts the range from $166,800 to $200,000 when printing costs are assumed in addition to the prv. These funds will be 
expended in the next fiscal year and will be included in the budget for 2020-21.

To the City Council of the City of San Mateo:

We, the undersigned, registered and qualified voters of the City of San Mateo (“City”),
present to the City Council this petition and request that the following proposed Ordinance
(“Measure”) be adopted without alteration or submitted to the registered and qualified
voters of the City for their adoption or rejection at the earliest regular election for which tt
qualifies pursuant to Section 1405 of the California Elections Code.

‘The full text of the Measure is as follows:

The People of the City of San Mateo do hereby ordain as follows:

Section 1. Purpose

The purpose of this Measute is to maintain the San Mateo General Plan so as to presetve the
livability and suburban character of the City of San Mateo by essentially maintaining,
through the year 2030, the height limits and densities first established by San Mateo voters in
1991, then amended and extended by the voters in 2004, but that would otherwise expire i
2020, while providing for the level of economic growth projected in the San Mateo General
Plan and, in a manner consistent with requirements of law, increasing the City’s commitment
to providing its fair share of affordable housing.

Section 2. Findings
The people of San Mateo find and declare:

A. The City of San Mateo is a mature community whose established and stable suburban
character provides an economic asset for its homeowners and residents, and whose diversity
is a soutce of pride.

B. ‘The City of San Mateo is already a well-balanced community with a strong mix of
residential, commercial and retail development. ‘The City provides a wide range of housing
opportunities for its residents. Currently, more than 44% of San Mateo’s housing units are
multi-family.

C. In 1990 the City Council adopted a General Plan for the City of San Mateo. The General
Plan assumed that it was desirable for the City to meet a projected level of growth which was
“estimated” or “anticipated” by the year 2005.

D. However, in 1991, the voters of the City of San Mateo determined that the 1990 General
Plan designated land uses, building intensities and population densities which would allow
far mote growth than was projected, and more growth than was desirable.

282 of 328


--- page 283 ---
EB. In 1991, the voters found that while the excess densities and intensities permissible under
the 1990 General Plan were intended to allow for flexibility, and were to be reserved for
projects which provided substantial public benefits, including affordable housing; the City
Council at that time had approved projects in the higher range of building intensity and
population density which had not provided sufficient public benefits or affordable housing.

F. In 1991, the voters determined that high-rise and high-density developments threatened
the viability of the valued suburban chatacter of the community and did not have the
support of San Mateo residents. The votets found that continued approval of such projects
would irrevocably change the character of San Mateo for the worse, and cause serious
adverse impacts to its citizens, in the form of increased traffic congestion, ait pollution and
noise levels, loss of views and other adverse visual impacts, reduced emergency services and
other public facilities, increased costs of government, and reduced quality of life.

G. In 1991, the voters determined that it was necessary to limit the maximum density and
intensity of development in San Mateo to levels which will eliminate or mitigate the impacts
set forth above. At the same time, the voters determined that such restrictions should not
unduly impair the City’s ability to achieve its economic development goals; and that it was of
utmost importance that such limitations not reduce the amount of affordable housing
production.

H. In 1991, the voters determined that the City Council had not included any mandatory
inclusionary housing or other programs in the 1990 General Plan to ensure the production
of affordable housing, and that it was necessary to increase the City’s commitment to
production of affordable housing.

I. In 1991, the voters found that the initiative Measure H and the General Plan amendments
adopted by it: encouraged the production of San Mateo’s fair share of housing affordable to
persons defined in Health and Safety Code section 50093; did not and. would not impose any
numerical or percentage limit on the development of housing units in the City of San Mateo;
encouraged the development of affordable housing units withm City limits; and was
intended to be and was consistent with Chapter 4.2 of Title 7 of the Government Code,
including Government Code section 65913.1.

J. In 1991, the voters found that the initiative Measure H and the General Plan amendments
adopted by it were intended to and did retain the General Plan, as amended, as an internally
consistent and legally adequate General Plan.

Section 3. General Plan Amendments
A. Background: General Plan Amendments

1) Section 3 of initiative Measure H set forth specific amendments to the San Mateo
General Plan as adopted by the San Mateo City Council im July 1990 (hereafter referred to as
“General Plan”). The General Plan was then amended to incorporate the amendments set
forth in Measure H.

2) Section 3 of initiative Measure P set forth specific additional amendments to the General
Plan, which was amended to incorporate the amendments set forth in Measure P.

3) This section of this imitative sets forth amendments to the General Plan that were
originally adopted by San Mateo voters in 1991, amended and re-adopted and extended
through the year 2020, and that will continue to be included within the General Plan through
the yeat 2030.

General Plan was Developed”) are amended and matntamed to read:

In November 1991, the voters adopted an initiative which amended. the General Plan.
The initiative made several changes to the General Plan, primarily directed at
reducing maximum heights and densities for residential and most non-residential
uses, while increasing the City’s commitment to providing affordable housing.

A comprehensive update of the General Plan, consistent with the provisions of

Measure H, was approved by the City Council in1996.

b. The text following Program H 2.10 (“Housing Densities”), are maintained to read:

One means of increasing housing potential is through redesignation of commercially
zoned and lower density residential properties to multi-family land use. The
redesignations approved in Policy H 2.10 will increase the potential for construction
of new units.

San Mateo’s multi-family zoning districts allow relatively high denstties in an effort to
encoutage the production of housing. In 1989, the R-3 District (the lowest density
multi-family zoning district) allowed up to 43 units per acre. Prior to the amendments
necessaty to make them conform to the initiative adopted by the voters in November
1991, the R-4 District allowed up to 58 units per acre and the R-5 District allowed up
to 124 units per acre. However, very few projects wete built up to the maximum
allowable densities. On average, most developments achieved between one-third and
one-half the allowable densities in these zoning districts, due to other constraints
such as parking, open space requirements and the costs of high-tise building
construction or multiple floors of underground parking.

The high range of allowable densities permitted by the zoning districts can result in
property owners over-valuing their properties based on unrealistic development
expectations. This in turn results in properties remaining undeveloped or reduces the
affordability of units constructed with inflated land prices. It can also render density
bonuses for affordable housing production useless.

on Program H 2.12 (“Mixed-Use”) is maintained to read:

A. The General Plan and all of its elements and parts may be reviewed and amended

inconsistent with the purposes, intent, or operative provisions of this mitiattve, including,
but not necessarily limited to, provisions reducing maximum height limits and densities for
specified uses.

B. Should the City Council determine that it is impossible to comply with the
requirements of state law without amending the General Plan in a manner inconsistent with
the purposes, intent, ot operative provisions of this initiative, it shall first seek voter approval
of any proposed inconsistent amendments. Failing this, it shall then seek appropriate judicial
relief.

Section 5. Implementation /No Unconstitutional ‘Taking

This initiative is not intended, and shall not be applied or construed, to authorize the City to
exercise its powers in a manner which will take private property for public use without the
payment of just compensation, but shall be interpreted, applied and implemented so as to
accomplish its purposes to the maximum constitutionally permissible extent. If application
of this initiative to a specific property of record as of its effective date would create a taking,
then the City Council may allow additional density ot uses on said property, upon findings
that the level of additional development permitted is the minimum necessary to avoid a
taking, and no lesser level of development would be sufficient to avoid a taking.

Such findings shall be based on full environmental review and economic feasibility studies
which ate circulated in the same manner as Draft Environmental Impact Reports, and must
be supported by a preponderance of the evidence.

Section 6. Severability

_ Ifany portion of this initiative is hereafter determined to be mvalid by a court of competent
jurisdiction, all remaining portions of this initiative shall remain in full force and effect. Hach

section, subsection, sentence, phrase, patt, or portion of this initiative would have been
adopted and passed irrespective of the fact that any one or more sections, subsections,
sentences, phrases, parts of portions be declared invalid or unconstitutional.

Section 7. Effective Date and Duration
A. The provisions of this initiative shall remain in effect through the year 2030.

B. This initiative shall take effect 10 days after the city council declares the results of the
election approving this measute.

Conflicting Ballot Measures

Section 8.

21


--- page 302 ---
In the event that this Measure and another measure or measures relating to the same or
similar subject matter shall appear on the same election ballot, the provisions of the other
measures shall be deemed in conflict with this measure. In the event that this Measure shall
receive a greater number of affirmative votes, the provisions of this Measure shall prevail in
their entirety, and the provisions of the other measure ot measures shall be null and void.

RESOLUTION NO. __ (2020) 
 
CALLING A GENERAL MUNICIPAL ELECTION ON TUESDAY, NOVEMBER 3, 2020  
FOR THE PURPOSE OF SUBMITTING A PROPOSED ORDINANCE TO AMEND THE CITY OF SAN MATEO GENERAL 
PLAN TO EXTEND FOR TEN YEARS THE EXPIRATION DATE FOR VOTER-ENACTED POLICIES LIMITING BUILDING 
HEIGHTS, RESIDENTIAL DENSITIES, AND NONRESIDENTIAL BUILDING INTENSITIES, AND TO MODIFY POLICIES 
ESTABLISHING AN INCLUSIONARY HOUSING REQUIREMENT FOR RESIDENTIAL RENTAL HOUSING PROJECTS 
 
WHEREAS, pursuant to authority provided by statute a petition has been filed with the legislative 
body of the City of San Mateo, California, signed by more than 10 per cent of the number of registered voters of 
the city to submit a proposed ordinance relating to extending the general plan policies enacted by Measure P in 
2004, which amended and extended the 1991 citizen’s initiative Measure H; and 
 
WHEREAS, Measure P extended the expiration provision until December 31, 2020; and 
 
WHEREAS, the County Elections Department at the request of the City Clerk examined the records of 
registration and ascertained that the petition is signed by the requisite number of voters, and has so certified; 
and 
 
WHEREAS, the City Council is authorized and directed by statute to submit the proposed ordinance 
to the voters; 
 
NOW, THEREFORE, THE CITY COUNCIL OF THE CITY OF SAN MATEO, CALIFORNIA, DOES RESOLVE, 
DECLARE, DETERMINE AND ORDER AS FOLLOWS: 
 
SECTION 1. 
That pursuant to the requirements of the laws of the State of California relating to 
charter cities, there is called and ordered to be held in the City of San Mateo, California, on Tuesday, November 
3, 2020, a General Municipal Election for the purpose of submitting the following proposed ordinance: 
 
Shall the proposed ordinance to amend the City of San 
Mateo General Plan to maintain for ten years voter-
enacted policies limiting building heights, residential 
densities, and nonresidential building intensities, and to 
modify and maintain for 10 years an inclusionary housing 
requirement for residential projects, be adopted?

To the City Council of the City of San Mateo:

We, the undersigned, registered and qualified voters of the City of San Mateo (“City”),
present to the City Council this petition and request that the following proposed Ordinance
(“Measure”) be adopted without alteration or submitted to the registered and qualified
voters of the City for their adoption or rejection at the earliest regular election for which tt
qualifies pursuant to Section 1405 of the California Elections Code.

‘The full text of the Measure is as follows:

The People of the City of San Mateo do hereby ordain as follows:

Section 1. Purpose

The purpose of this Measute is to maintain the San Mateo General Plan so as to presetve the
livability and suburban character of the City of San Mateo by essentially maintaining,
through the year 2030, the height limits and densities first established by San Mateo voters in
1991, then amended and extended by the voters in 2004, but that would otherwise expire i
2020, while providing for the level of economic growth projected in the San Mateo General
Plan and, in a manner consistent with requirements of law, increasing the City’s commitment
to providing its fair share of affordable housing.

Section 2. Findings
The people of San Mateo find and declare:

A. The City of San Mateo is a mature community whose established and stable suburban
character provides an economic asset for its homeowners and residents, and whose diversity
is a soutce of pride.

B. ‘The City of San Mateo is already a well-balanced community with a strong mix of
residential, commercial and retail development. ‘The City provides a wide range of housing
opportunities for its residents. Currently, more than 44% of San Mateo’s housing units are
multi-family.

C. In 1990 the City Council adopted a General Plan for the City of San Mateo. The General
Plan assumed that it was desirable for the City to meet a projected level of growth which was
“estimated” or “anticipated” by the year 2005.

D. However, in 1991, the voters of the City of San Mateo determined that the 1990 General
Plan designated land uses, building intensities and population densities which would allow
far mote growth than was projected, and more growth than was desirable.

305 of 328


--- page 306 ---
EB. In 1991, the voters found that while the excess densities and intensities permissible under
the 1990 General Plan were intended to allow for flexibility, and were to be reserved for
projects which provided substantial public benefits, including affordable housing; the City
Council at that time had approved projects in the higher range of building intensity and
population density which had not provided sufficient public benefits or affordable housing.

F. In 1991, the voters determined that high-rise and high-density developments threatened
the viability of the valued suburban chatacter of the community and did not have the
support of San Mateo residents. The votets found that continued approval of such projects
would irrevocably change the character of San Mateo for the worse, and cause serious
adverse impacts to its citizens, in the form of increased traffic congestion, ait pollution and
noise levels, loss of views and other adverse visual impacts, reduced emergency services and
other public facilities, increased costs of government, and reduced quality of life.

G. In 1991, the voters determined that it was necessary to limit the maximum density and
intensity of development in San Mateo to levels which will eliminate or mitigate the impacts
set forth above. At the same time, the voters determined that such restrictions should not
unduly impair the City’s ability to achieve its economic development goals; and that it was of
utmost importance that such limitations not reduce the amount of affordable housing
production.

H. In 1991, the voters determined that the City Council had not included any mandatory
inclusionary housing or other programs in the 1990 General Plan to ensure the production
of affordable housing, and that it was necessary to increase the City’s commitment to
production of affordable housing.

I. In 1991, the voters found that the initiative Measure H and the General Plan amendments
adopted by it: encouraged the production of San Mateo’s fair share of housing affordable to
persons defined in Health and Safety Code section 50093; did not and. would not impose any
numerical or percentage limit on the development of housing units in the City of San Mateo;
encouraged the development of affordable housing units withm City limits; and was
intended to be and was consistent with Chapter 4.2 of Title 7 of the Government Code,
including Government Code section 65913.1.

J. In 1991, the voters found that the initiative Measure H and the General Plan amendments
adopted by it were intended to and did retain the General Plan, as amended, as an internally
consistent and legally adequate General Plan.

K. In 2004, the voters approved. Measure P, to amend and extend until 2020 the General
Plan amendments adopted by Measure H, finding that the findings and determinations made

by the voters in adopting Measure H in 1991 continued to reflect the views and desires of

306 of 328


--- page 307 ---
the people of San Mateo and should continue to serve to guide the development of the
community through the year 2020.

L. In 2018, the findings and determinations made by San Mateo voters in 1991 in adopting
initiative Measute H, and in 2004 in adopting initiative Measure P, and the General Plan
amendments approved by those measures, continue to reflect the views and desires of the
people of San Mateo and should continue to setve to guide the development of the
community through the year 2030. —

M. In 2018, the further updates, clarifications and changes to Measure P contained in this
Measute maintain all significant, substantive portions of the voter-adopted initiative Measure
P, while clarifying references to building height limits for certain residential land use
categories, and updating inclusionary housing requirements consistent with law.

Section 3. General Plan Amendments
A. Background: General Plan Amendments

1) Section 3 of initiative Measure H set forth specific amendments to the San Mateo
General Plan as adopted by the San Mateo City Council im July 1990 (hereafter referred to as
“General Plan”). The General Plan was then amended to incorporate the amendments set
forth in Measure H.

2) Section 3 of initiative Measure P set forth specific additional amendments to the General
Plan, which was amended to incorporate the amendments set forth in Measure P.

3) This section of this imitative sets forth amendments to the General Plan that were
originally adopted by San Mateo voters in 1991, amended and re-adopted and extended
through the year 2020, and that will continue to be included within the General Plan through
the yeat 2030.

Measure H, was approved by the City Council in1996.

floor area ratio (FAR) (the gross floor area of a building divided by the net lot area) to

oo commercial areas.

b. The text following Program H 2.10 (“Housing Densities”), are maintained to read:

One means of increasing housing potential is through redesignation of commercially
zoned and lower density residential properties to multi-family land use. The
redesignations approved in Policy H 2.10 will increase the potential for construction
of new units.

San Mateo’s multi-family zoning districts allow relatively high denstties in an effort to
encoutage the production of housing. In 1989, the R-3 District (the lowest density
multi-family zoning district) allowed up to 43 units per acre. Prior to the amendments
necessaty to make them conform to the initiative adopted by the voters in November
1991, the R-4 District allowed up to 58 units per acre and the R-5 District allowed up
to 124 units per acre. However, very few projects wete built up to the maximum
allowable densities. On average, most developments achieved between one-third and
one-half the allowable densities in these zoning districts, due to other constraints
such as parking, open space requirements and the costs of high-tise building
construction or multiple floors of underground parking.

The high range of allowable densities permitted by the zoning districts can result in
property owners over-valuing their properties based on unrealistic development
expectations. This in turn results in properties remaining undeveloped or reduces the
affordability of units constructed with inflated land prices. It can also render density
bonuses for affordable housing production useless.

F.

Many things can be done to strengthen major focal points. The Downtown Specific
Plan includes requirements for ground floor retail, and the General Plan permits
mixed uses and building heights of up to 75 feet in the Downtown. Hillsdale Mall
could be strengthened by higher floor area ratios (the ratio of building floor area. to
Jot area), visible retail uses (outward focusing), and a more consistent architectural or
landscape treatment. The office development along SR 92 could be enhanced by
permitting buildings up to 75 feet in height, altering higher floor area. ratios and
architectural and landscape treatments. Focal points can be discouraged in the middle
sections of Fl Camino Real (SR 82) and many other commercial zones by changing
the zoning to permit no high-rises or buildings with excessive bulk.

Section 4. Subsequent General Plan Amendments

A. The General Plan and all of its elements and parts may be reviewed and amended

pursuant to Policy LU 6-2 and as may be required to conform to state law, without

pe limitation, except that the City Council may not amend the General Plan-in aimanner-~- ~ ~~ >>>

323 of 328


--- page 324 ---
inconsistent with the purposes, intent, or operative provisions of this mitiattve, including,
but not necessarily limited to, provisions reducing maximum height limits and densities for
specified uses.

B. Should the City Council determine that it is impossible to comply with the
requirements of state law without amending the General Plan in a manner inconsistent with
the purposes, intent, ot operative provisions of this initiative, it shall first seek voter approval
of any proposed inconsistent amendments. Failing this, it shall then seek appropriate judicial
relief.

Section 5. Implementation /No Unconstitutional ‘Taking

This initiative is not intended, and shall not be applied or construed, to authorize the City to
exercise its powers in a manner which will take private property for public use without the
payment of just compensation, but shall be interpreted, applied and implemented so as to
accomplish its purposes to the maximum constitutionally permissible extent. If application
of this initiative to a specific property of record as of its effective date would create a taking,
then the City Council may allow additional density ot uses on said property, upon findings
that the level of additional development permitted is the minimum necessary to avoid a
taking, and no lesser level of development would be sufficient to avoid a taking.

Such findings shall be based on full environmental review and economic feasibility studies
which ate circulated in the same manner as Draft Environmental Impact Reports, and must
be supported by a preponderance of the evidence.

Section 6. Severability

_ Ifany portion of this initiative is hereafter determined to be mvalid by a court of competent
jurisdiction, all remaining portions of this initiative shall remain in full force and effect. Hach

section, subsection, sentence, phrase, patt, or portion of this initiative would have been
adopted and passed irrespective of the fact that any one or more sections, subsections,
sentences, phrases, parts of portions be declared invalid or unconstitutional.

Section 7. Effective Date and Duration
A. The provisions of this initiative shall remain in effect through the year 2030.

B. This initiative shall take effect 10 days after the city council declares the results of the
election approving this measute.

Conflicting Ballot Measures

Section 8.

324 of 328

--- page 325 ---
In the event that this Measure and another measure or measures relating to the same or
similar subject matter shall appear on the same election ballot, the provisions of the other
measures shall be deemed in conflict with this measure. In the event that this Measure shall
receive a greater number of affirmative votes, the provisions of this Measure shall prevail in
their entirety, and the provisions of the other measure ot measures shall be null and void.
